Dublin, September 29th.

"The counties of Kilkenny and Carlow, are again in a very agitated state; numbers of missionaries being sent by Holt into those counties, for the purpose of stirring up the deluded peasantry; and he has already, in a great measure, but too well succeeded."

"This day thirty-seven of the Fermanagh militia arrived in town from the county of Wicklow; they were all deprived of their arms, accoutrements, &c. by the rebels."*

[* See p. 159.]

"The Irish papers say, 'It is astonishing what a head the rebel Holt is making.' We presume this is in allusion to the 300Z. offered for it."*—Courier Newspaper, 4th October, 1798.

[* See pages 151, 152.]

"On Saturday last, several of the yeomanry corps of the county of Wicklow, who had been put off permanent duty for some time past, were again called on, and are to continue on service until further orders. They consider this a fortunate circumstance, as they believe they are thus more secure against the rebels. While they were off duty, it is certain that numbers of them were killed in their houses. Those corps arc principally cavalry. They patrol the hills at night; but they do little good; for in most parts of the country, the ground is so rocky and rough, or so intersected with pits, &c. that no horsemen can venture out of the high road.* The rebels know the country and find safety in dispersion; while the force against them, to be safe, must keep itself together."—Courier Newspaper, 8th October, 1798.

[* See p. 259.]

Page 346

"The Irish papers state that Lord Meath lately returned with his corps to his seat near Bray, after having minutely searched every little hole and cranny in the mountains of Wicklow for the rebel Holt and his army."—Courier Newspaper, 5th October, 1798.

Dublin, October 1st.

"The most savage barbarities and wanton cruelties still continue to be committed in the county of Wicklow. All parties seem to vid with each other in acts of atrocity."*

[*See pages 209 to 211, and 216 to 222.]

Every large mansion, which report destines for a barrack, is destroyed by the rebels, together with the houses of all those who are inimical to their views.*]

[* See pages 177, 229. &c.]

"On the other hand, such is the sanguinary system pursued, that if it is much longer continued, that once populous and industrious county must be entirely depopulated. Protections are disregarded."

"The rebel leader Holt has not yet been captured; in defiance of the very numerous forces which have marched against him in every direction, he still continues to lay the country under contribution with impunity. And this circumstance occasions no small surprise here. It can scarcely be accounted for reasonably, how such a daring miscreant can possibly elude the vigilance of the large force which has been sent against him."

[1 Oct 1798]

"It is reported this evening, that general orders have been given to the officers commanding in WiCklow, to act with the utmost severity in that county."—Courier Newspaper, 6th October, 1798.

Page 347

Dublin, October 2nd.

"A report has been some days in circulation of general orders being sent to the officers commanding in the county of Wicklow, to proceed against the rebels with the utmost severity. By the melancholy accounts which have arrived to-day from that dreadful scene of carnage, it appears that such order was sent, and has since been acted upon."

"In the course of this evening, a number of families have arrived at the inns in Kevin Street, from different quarters of that county; happy in having escaped with their lives, though most of them beggared, their property having been destroyed."

"A report prevailed this evening, that nine pieces of cannon, and five ammunition waggons, fell into the hands of the rebels yesterday near Rathcoole, in the county of Kildare."

"Every hour brings some new account of assassination and carnage, which by their frequency seem to have already deadened every feeling of humanity, and steeled the heart against every impulse of compassion. The most shocking enormities are daily committed by the rebels against the yeomanry, and the Protestant Orange families in Wicklow and the neighbouring counties. These enormities are committed not by an army of rebels, but by detached parties assembling in the dead of night perhaps, and dispersing when the business of murder is dispatched; to prevent them, has been thought impossible—to punish them in a legal way, impracticable. Nor is it Holt and his followers alone, who have dealt in those murderous deeds. The savage spirit has infected the mass of the peasantry; who, notwithstanding the leniency of his Excellency, seem to consider themselves as a forlorn hope in an enemy's country, and think only of wreaking vengeance on that enemy—the only means they possess of doing so, are the firebrand and the pike, and of these they cruelly avail themselves!"*—Courier Newspaper, 8lh October, 1798.

[* See pages 209 to 211, aad 216 to 222.]

Page 348

Dublin, Thursday October 4th.

[2 Oct 1798]

Yesterday morning, the Cork mail was attacked be tween Redgap and Kilcullen, by about fifty armed rebels, commanded by a fellow named O'Neil. Two dragoons who attended the mail coach, made a most gallant defence, but were at length overpowered and the mail carried off, the passengers, guards, coachman, and dragoons, were permitted to continue their journey without further injury or interruption."

"Three deserters from the King's county militia, who had joined Holt,* and who bad been taken prisoners in a late skirmish in the county Wicklow, were yesterday brought into town under a strong guard and lodged in the barrack. It is expected they will be tried immediately by court martial."

[* See page 204.]

"Holt has threatened a visit to Dundrum, and its inhabitants are in constant and fearful expectations of his putting the threat into execution; every night a portion of them keep watch, and on Sunday night, fearing the visit would then be paid, every person in the village, armed and unarmed, held himself in readiness to repel the attack."—Courier Newspaper, 9th October, 1798.

[3 Oct 1798]

"Of the conduct of a party of his [Holt's] men, I have the following authentic anecdote:—They had stopped one of the mail coaches (I believe the Cork) some time back at Kilcullen, and having brought the passengers, one of whom was a Mr. Dudgeon, of this city, a mercer, into the inn, they swore thera as to the fact of their not being Orangemen, and having been satisBed by the answers given, the person who seemed to command the rest, and to whom a very marked deference was paid, addressed the passengers in terms rather polite, and said, that a want of shoes and boots, which was experienced by the party, obliged him to lay their purses under contribution—the affrighted captives accordingly laid the several sums of money which they had about them on the table, and were agreeably surprised on beholding the rebel leader take a guinea only from each sum! Mr. Dudgeon's purse alone contained sixty guineas. This instance of moderation, in what are termed banditti, and said to be connected for the purpose of rapine, I could not bring myself to credit, but that it has been had from Mr. Dudgeon's own mouth."—Courier Newspaper, 11th October, 1798.

Page 349

"Neil, who at the head of a considerable body of rebels robbed the Cork mail, a few nights ago, was sometime since in the service of Richard Frizell, Jun., Esquire, of Rathfarnham, a member of the college corps, from whose house he absconded at the commencement of the rebellion, in company with the rebels that left the above town. The family being from home, he took with him Mr. Frizell's uniform and accoutrements, a sword, and several other articles of value. Neil behaved with great humanity to the passengers in the coach, protecting their persons from injury, and giving them passes, signed 'Michael Neil, Captain, Roundwood Cavalry.' He for some time acted in conjunction with Holt, but disagreeing with him about the share of the booty he was entitled to, drew off' his party, and now with a daring band of free-booters, adds another to the numerous gangs that infest the county of Wicklow."

[4 Oct 1798]

"Thursday night. Major Sirr apprehended a person who is a pretended recruiting sergeant, named Boyle and his accomplice, one Laurence Kelly, charged with circulating counterfeit guinea bank notes. The Major, on searching Boyle, found upon him fifty-six of those fictitious notes, which were counterfeits upon the National Bank, and Sir Thomas Lighten and Co.

"The comrade of Boyle said he saw him give ten guineas in genuine notes for fourteen of them, to a person named Hacket, in search of which latter person. Major Sirr immediately proceeded, but he was flown. Both these men were lodged in prison, one of whom, Kelly, pretended to be a very simple person. But when, he came before Mr. Gregg the jailer, he recognised him to be an intimate with offenders in the new prison."—Courier Newspaper, 13th October, 1798.

Page 350

Dublin, 6th October.

[6 Oct 1798]

"Such is the daring pitch to which the insurgents belonging to Holt's party has arisen, that early this morning, a large body of them paid a visit to Alderman Exshaw's demesne at Roebuck, within a few miles of Dublin, and carried off fourteen wethers belonging to him. He has offered a reward of ten guineas for the apprehension of any of those depredators."

Account of Holt and his Party, {given in a Letter from Dublin.)

"Scarce a day passes without accounts being received of some outrage and murder committed in the counties of Wicklow and Kildare, and sometimes in the county of Dublin. Holt's party is increasing every day, and becoming more formidable in number and desperation. Since toy last, more wounded soldiers have been brought in from the county of Wicklow, principally composed of the Fermanagh militia. The best ascertained accounts of Holt's force, state it to consist of seven or eight hundred men, all composed of deserters from different regiments of militia, and who are said to be sworn in a compact of death to each other, sooner than yield or separate. The consciousness that they cannot hope for pardon renders them desperate and ferocious to the last degree.*

[* See p. 250.]

"It is matter of no small wonder the influence which this man has attained over his followers, when his situation hitherto, and habits of life are considered. For many years he lived as gardener with several families in the county of Wicklow, and while in the service of a Mr. Sweney, a magistrate, his master was accustomed to make him special, to execute writs, &c.*

[* See p. 2.]

"This was not an office, it is to be supposed, to recommend him to much popular affection; latterly he lived with the late lord mayor of this city, Alderman Fleming, from whose service he was discharged, in consequence of some very wanton cruelty, exercised on one of the alderman's horses; he then returned to the county of Wicklow, where his local knowledge of the country induced some of his former employers to have him appointed a barony constable, and in this situation he was, when the rebellion furnished him with an opportunity of displaying that courage which he possesses in a very great degree, and those natural talents which are generally ascribed to him."—Courier Newspaper, 11th October, 1798.

Page 351

Dublin, October 9.

[6 Oct 1798]

"The murders, in the county of Wicklow, continue with unabated fury. The rebel chief. Holt, has not yet been taken. General Crawford, on Saturday last, sent into town all the field pieces which were intended to act against the rebels at Wicklow, finding them quite useless in his operations on the mountains, against the foe opposed to him, whose chief strength consists in the situation of the country."

"It is with heartfelt concern we announce the death of Captain Hume, one of the members of Parliament for the county of Wicklow, who was killed in, an action which his corps has had with Holt's ferocious banditti.* He had been all the preceding night at the head of his corps, and a part of the army in search of Holt, when returning home to take some rest, and considerably advanced in front of his party, when he came up with some of Holt's party, whom he mistook for some of his friends, as they were dressed in the military fashion, and was shot through the body and head. The murderers then effected their escape to the mountains."—Dublin Evening Post.

[* See pages 251, 252, 253.]

[8 Oct 1798]

"Yesterday, several prisoners, taken in skirmishes with some of Holt's parties, were brought into town under an escort of the Royal Irish Dragoons."—Courier Newspaper, 13th October, 1798.

Page 352

Dublin, October 11.

The following is the account given in Saunders's News-Letter of the death of Mr. Hume:—"In consequence of information, that a number of the banditti were on the mountains in his neighbourhood. Captain Hume, and the corps of yeomanry under his Command, had spent the preceding night in pursuit, and had killed one and made three prisoners. Either to reconnoitre, or to obtain refreshment. Captain Hume, unattended, had separated from his troop, whom he left at a considerable distance. During this absence, he perceived some men on foot, in uniform, and on a part of the mountain where he left his corps, between whom and those persons was a deep and wide ravine. Appearing like yeomen, Captain Hume rode towards them, and unhappily, under this delusion, advanced quite close before it was possible to undeceive himself. One of the desperate villains, who was dressed in an officer's outside coat, at this moment drew forth a pistol, the baU from which entered the Captain's side, and being brought to the ground by the wound, another of the party shot him through the head in view of his own corps, who thus saw their officer massacred without its being possible, from their distance, to afford that instant aid which his situation rendered necessary. The villains then mounted the horse of the deceased, and rode him for some time, but from the ruggedness of the mountain, and difficulty of accent, they afterwards abandoned him, and effected their escape, though immediately pursued by Captain Hume's men, carrying off the holster pistols of the unfortunate gentleman."—Courier Newspaper, 15th October, 1798.

Page 353

London, Tuesday, October 16.

"The intelligence received from Ireland by this day's mail, is of tliat melancholy kind, the continuance of which we have so often had occasion to lament, and which, we are afraid, it will be too frequently our lot to detail during the ensuing winter. The counties of Wicklow and Wexford are still a prey to all the horrors of the most cruel civil warfare. The insurgents in these counties, whether rendered desperate by their hopeless situation, or stimulated by revenge and the desire of plunder, remain in force, notwithstanding the considerable bodies of troops that act against them. They appear to be formidable, not so much on account of their numbers, as their enterprise, and above all, the mountainous nature of that part of the country, with the fastnesses of which they are intimately acquainted."

Extract of a Letter from Waterford, October 9.

"The predatory outrages of Holt, Hacket, and their gangs in the counties of Wicklow and Wexford, continue with a degree of barbarism and ferocity, disgraceful and destructive to the nation where they are carried on, and so far are those gangs from being intimidated by the forces already sent against them, that their numbers and their crimes daily increased

Page 354

"The fastnesses of those blood-stained miscreants, are situated in a range of wild mountains, for a chain of above twenty-five miles, and the scenes of their depredations are in the vicinity of those mountains.

"Twenty thousand troops would be insufficient to form a cordon to surround them, and so long as there are cattle, com, or any other species of sustenance to be procured by plunder, within their range, they cannot be starved out. The. mountains, rocks, and ravines, among which they range, and with which they are familiarly acquainted, afford them numberless posts of security and facilities for escape, inaccessible to cavalry or artillery, and in which a perfect knowledge of the country and the means of obtaining subsistence, render them an overmatch for ten times their number of regular troops, before a twentieth part of whom they would 'not stand five minutes in regular action.

"By day they sleep in caverns or cottages, or concert operations of murder and robbery for the night, when they disperse themselves in parties, too numerous and formidable for the resistance of single families, on whom they pounce like Indians, and plunder and murder without mercy. To be a Protestant, because the supposed enemy to anarchy, or to be a yeoman, because the open and manly champion of loyalty, the constitution and the laws, are in their miscreant eyes, crimes, the most unpardonable, and therefore doom indiscriminately persons of that description to butchery.

[10 Oct 1798]

"Yesterday, a considerable force marched from town to the county of Wicklow, to join the forces already sent against those ferocious miscreants. Most of the yeomanry corps who had received a temporary suspension from permanent duty, have again solicited to be-called forward, in order to have an opportunity of contributing their gallant aid for the extermination of those savages, and from their intimate knowledge of the country, they are calculated to render the most effectual services.

Page 355

It is too much to be apprehended, that the country people afford these miscreants an asylum in their cabins, and in turn participate their spoils, but government is now determined to exert the roost vigorous and indefatigable force for the extirpation of those brigands, and will send troops to traverse every perch of the Wexford and Wicklow mountains until they are completely cut off."—Courier Newspaper, 16th October, 1798.

"The Dublin Journal says—'Beside that of Mr. Hume, the following murders have been within these few days committed in the county of Wicklow. On the 6th instant, a poor man named Cooke, sent by the agent of a nobleman to serve some legal notices on his estate near Hollywood, was barbarously murdered. One Thornton, a land surveyor, near Stratford on Slaney, was taken on the same morning out of his bed, carried naked two miles to a place called Gibstown-, and cruelly massacred, he has left a widow and several wretched orphans. A farmer named Stewart, and another named Synnot, were both murdered on the same day; the first near Hacket's-town, the second near Baltinglass. All these poor men were Protestants.'"

"Late on Wednesday night, Holt and a large party of his fellow-traitors, had the audacity to attack a small detachment of the Argyle Fencibles, and Captain Hornidge's Talbotstown Cavalry, at a place called Glanbride, in the county Wicklow; an action commenced which lasted until next day-break, in the event of which nineteen of the rebels, and a sergeant of the Fencibles were killed. Holt himself is supposed to have been dangerously wounded, but escaped with the remainder of his party."*—Dublin Journal.—Courier Newspaper, 18th October, 1798.

[* This is the affair detailed by Holt at pages 266 to 270.]

Page 356

Dublin, October 15.

"The daring and desperate ravager Holt, after having so long set the powers of government at defiance, and rioted in the blood and plunder of the loyal inhabitants of the counties of Wicklow, Wexford, Carlow, and Kildare, has, we hear, recently had the audacity to send proposals of capitulation to government, for the pardon of himself and his gang, finding such measures are on foot as rendered his destruction utterly inevitable, but those proposals have been treated with the contempt they deserve. Such decisive steps will be immediately taken towards this sanguinary miscreant and his followers, as well as towards the numerous petty gangs, who have for some time past, perpetrated numberless murders and robberies in his name, as must by a terrible example convince wretches of their description, that the justice of the country around cannot long with impunity be trifled with."—Hibernian Telegraph—Courier Newspaper, 19th October, 1798.

Dublin, October 18.

[11 Oct 1798]

"Early on Thursday morning, a detachment of the Duke of York's Highlanders, commanded by Major Meredith, fell in with Holt and his party, at a village near the glen of Imail.* The darkness of the morning favoured the escape of the rebels, except seventeen who were killed, and one wounded—from the latter, who was made prisoner, some useful information has been obtained. Holt was shot through the thigh, and wounded in the foot, but contrived to crawl to some distance from the scene of action, when meeting with a horse he was just able to mount, and thus for the present has eluded the punishment that awaits him."—Courier Newspaper, 22d October, 1798.

[* See pages 265 and 277.]]

Page 357

[16 Oct 1798]

"On Tuesday last, in the county of Wicklow at mid-day, a banditti had the audacity to attack the house and offices of William Tomlinson, Esq., Lieutenant in the Rathdrum cavalry. They set fire to the out-offices, which were entirely consumed, and also attempted to set fire to the dwelling-house, but fortunately had not time to effect their diabolical purpose. The attack was made while Mr. Tomlinson was absent with his troop and a part of the army, in pursuit of Holt and his fellow-ruffians in the mountains. Mr. Tomlinson's loss is the more to be regretted, as he is a most active officer, and highly distinguished for\is loyal and spirited exertions to suppress the rebellion existing in that part of the country."—Dublin Journal.—Courier Newspaper, 27th October, 1798.

Dublin, Saturday, October 20.

[18 Oct 1798]

"On Thursday, two men who were driving nine bullocks into town, and who said they came from Kilcullen, were stepped at the barrier, and while the sentinels were interrogating them, in that very moment a gentleman came up who claimed the bullocks as being his property, and recognised their quondam owners as persons who had been missing a long time, and supposed to be of Holt's party. The gentleman having established his claim, had his cattle returned to him, and the rebel drovers were committed to Kilmainham goal."—Courier Newspaper, 24th October, 1798.

Dublin, Saturday, October 20.

"On Thursday last an engagement look place about three miles from Rathdruiri, between a large body of rebels, and a October, part of the army under General Eustace. The General had only eighty men under him. The rebels were numerous, and commanded by a Captain of theirs called Dwyer.* He drew up his men in regular order of battle, they being mostly armed with muskets. The confest lasted but'a short time. General Eustace, in consequence of the great inferiority of his force, was obliged to retire ."—Courier Newspaper, 25tk October, 1798.

[* See note on page l57.]

Page 358

"One of the letters we have received, says, that the rebel leader Hacket is taken prisoner, and that there is every reason to expect that Holt will soon be completely subdued. Hacket was very active and fenterprizing; he was very successful in supplying Holt's army with recruits."

"By the recent juncture and active bravery of the military and yeomanry troops in the counties of Wicklow and Wexford, we are extremely happy to learn that a death-blow has been given to the sanguinary marauders under Holt, Hacket, and other desperate and. abandoned leaders, whom no tenders of mercy could induce to .abandon their rebellious pursuits, and who could have no other object but devastation and plunder. These barbarian hordes principally consisted of either the exasperated relatives of rebels who had fallen by the sword of war, or the hand of justice—or of miscreants' whose bloody atrocities were too notorious in the country to allow them any hope of eluding a just and terrible-vengeance—or of deserters from the king's standard, who could have no mercy to expect.

"Thus, those miscreants, making up their minds to the fall they must expect whenever they should come within the reach of justice, and cut off in practice as well as in inclination from every means of honest livelihood, sought a mutual protection in confederacy, and promised themselves the October, double advantage of gratifying a savage vengeance by murdering every loyal man who had been active in suppressing rebellion or punishing its abettors—or hunting down and plundering the defenceless habitations of the wealthy and peaceable, and thus realising for a time the primary objects of rebellion."—Hibernian Telegraph.—Courier Newspaper, 30th October, 1798.

Page 359

"The report of the rebel leader Hacket being taken appears doubtful. According to some accounts he effected his escape, though closely pursued by a corps of yeomanry, after a smart skirmish in which a number of his followers were killed.

"The following extract of a letter from Dublin, received by this mail, gives a very melancholy picture of the situation of the county of Wicklow.

[25 Oct 1798]

"The pastime of the county of Wicklow is shooting human creatures, but on a dearth of game that of burning cabins is resorted to; but these being difficult to find on Thursday 25 . night last in the neighbourhood of Roundwood, a set of those sportsmen, disappointed of the number they intended to shoot before supper, set fire to four Romish chapels. Next day the country people rose up, gave battle, and killed upwards of seven hundred of the ——, some of whom were the King's county ——, commanded by the noted A——. To be met on the road, even with a protection in your hand, is inevitable death; and to be found in your house is as certain; because in the former case you are running away; and in the latter you are hiding. You are not to think there is any exaggeration in this statement, I know the fact. Lord Cornwallis is no match for the Orange faction."—-Courier Newspaper, 31st October, 1798.

Dublin, October 27th.

"The famous Holt has, it is said, died of a fever which he caught in consequence of some severe wounds he received in a late engagement with a party of the king's forces."

Page 360

Dublin, October 29th.

"We have the pleasure to learn by letters from Wexford, that the town and part of the county, are perfectly restored to quietness and safety. Every stratagem has been used by Hacket, Holt, and other leaders of predatory gangs to induce the peasantry to join with them, but without effect. The last stratagem of this sort played off by Hacket, very nearly terminated in his own destruction; nine of his gang, out of a party consisting of himself and eleven others having been cut to pieces. Those miscreants have long owed their safety to the circumstance of their appearing in uniform, and armed in the same manner as the yeomanry corps of the country, from which circumstance they are often viewed at a distance as friends by the very troops in pursuit of them. Having failed in every attempt to induce the peasantry to rise and join them in the plunder and extermination of all the Protestants in the county, whom they style the Orange party, a number of them went on Sunday se'nnight [21st] to a chapel in the county, while the congregation were assembled, and drawing their swords, rushed at full gallop amongst them, whom they quickly dispersed, and then burnt the chapel.

"Their next step was to propagate a report that this feat had been done by a party of yeomen cavalry. Many of the gang, however, were recognised by the congregation during the outrage and known lo be rebels; the consequence of which was, their meeting-place and time near Killoughran wood, was made known to the officer commanding in the district, and the before mentioned number of Backet's gang cut to pieces."—Hibernian Telegraph.—Courier Newspaper, 1st November, 1798.

Dublin, October 30th.

"Engagements have lately taken place in the county of Wicklow; Holt is not taken, and that part of the country is yet much disturbed. Terror and danger are cause and effect: the latter must ever be apprehended where the former exists."—Courier Newspaper, 3rd November, 1798.

[3 Nov 1798]

Page 361

Dublin, November 3rd, in the evening.

"One of the sheriff's peers has reported that he received information in the course of this week that Holt, the rebel chief, was in this city, and he sought for Major Sirr, to assist in apprehending him; but the major was no where to be found, and the sheriff's peer being afraid 'to meet the formidable Holt, that terrible rebel escaped. It is also reported that Holt has contrived to have a letter conveyed to the Castle, directed to the Marquis Cornwallis, and containing a proposal to surrender on condition of his life being spared, and a condition to go whither he pleased out of his Majesty's dominions; and directing where an answer may be left. Threats of increased depredation were held out in the event of his proposal being rejected."*—Courier Newspaper, 7th November, 1798.

[* See pages 265 and 277.]

Dublin, 3rd November.

"Hacket's head is now fixed on a pike in a conspicuous place of the town of Gorey.* He was second in command to Holt. Information had been obtained that he and four of his gang were lurking in a wood near Gorey; several corps of yeomanry were collected, who begirt the wood and left no means of escape. Hacket and his party fought desperately' until their ammunition was quite expended; they were tlien surrounded. Three of them were killed. Hacket and another died fighting."

[* See pages 280, 362, and 366.]

[3 Nov 1798]

"A private letter, dated Dublin, Nov. 3rd, says—'To-day nothing of importance is abroad, except the circumstance of Holt being probably captured, or dead by this time, if I can believe one of the Ancient Britons, with whom I was speaking about half an hour ago, and who arrived in town from the part of the county of Wicklow, infested by that desperate chieftain and his banditti. The Ancient Briton told me that he left a considerable military force in such close and eager pursuit of Holt, that it was impossible he could escape, and that the horse he was then riding was one Holt had deserted, in making an hair-breadth escape into some fastness. This may be something in the line of 'men whose heads did grow beneath their shoulders,' and it may be the fact. The soldier also told me that they had taken a brother of Holt's, and lodged him in Rosborough house, a seat of the Earl of Miltown's, and now converted into a barrack for the king's troops."—Courier Newspaper, 8th November, 1798.

Page 362

"Holt, the self-created general of the insurgents in Wexford, and Wicklow, has written a letter to government in which he offers to come in on certain conditions being granted to him, and he concludes his letter with these impudent expressions—'If you will not agree to those terms, it is time for you to look to yourselves, as for me, I am not afraid to die.' His curious epistle is signed 'HOLT, General.'"

"It is not true, although so generally reported, that the head of Hacket, Holt's associate chieftain, has been brought in."—Courier Newspaper, 12th November, 1798.

Dublin, November 9th.

"Skirmishing still goes on in the county of Wicklow, and scarcely a day passes, but some men are 'killed off' on the side of one party or the other. The gentlemen of the county have applied to Lord Cornwallis for a military force to be stationed throughout that, county. Their request has ngs. been acquiesced into the fullest extent, and encampments November, are to be directly formed in a chain of telegraphic communication along the different heights in that county. A few days since, Mr. Morley Saunders, one of the candidates for the county, with three officers of the 89th, were looking at different situations best calculated for more encampments; they had gone into a cabin, when they heard some shots fired, which they concluded to be a signal for the knights of the pike to assemble. Nor was their conclusion erroneous. They mounted their horses, and before they had gone far they were fired on, and an exchange of shots took place, without however any injury being done on either side. All that night the king's troops had patroles scouring the country. Towards morning their advanced guard fell in with the advanced guard of the rebels; the troops fired, and the rebels precipitately retreated; some were killed, and about six of the insurgents taken prisoners. by the royal troops. The town of Stratford on Slaney, is one of the places determined. on by the military for an encampment; from its commanding situation (being on the top of a high hill) it is most admirably calculated for annoying the enemy."—Courier Newspaper, 13th November, 1798.

Page 363

London, Friday, November 16th.

"A mail from Dublin arrived this morning, with papers and letters of the 12th instant. The papers contain no intelligence, except a report that the celebrated Holt has surrendered himself to Lord Powerscourt, at Powerscourt house."*—Courier Newspaper, 16th November, 1798.

London, Saturday, November 17th.

"The intelligence we stated yesterday, relative to the surrender of Holt was perfectly correct. His arrival in Dublin is thus announced in the Evening Post of Tuesday last:

[11 Nov 1798]

"'Holt was this day brought to the Castle under a strong escort of the Powerscourt cavalry. He had previously surrendered to Lord Powerscourt, on terms it is said of transportation. Holt was dressed in a military uniform, scarlet faced with blue, and wore a military great coat,'"*—Courier Newspaper, 17th November, 1798.

[* See page 296.]

Page 364

"A letter from Dublin says—'Holt had long endeavoured to negociate for bis security, when he found his associates deserting him; and at last, when left almost alone, surrendered unconditionally, expressing at the same time a hope, that the discoveries and information which he should be enabled to communicate, might procure him the favour of government.'"

[12 Nov 1798]

"Since the surrender of Holt, vast numbers of rebels have voluntarily surrendered themselves in the counties of Wicklow, Wexford, and Kildare; in the latter county, on Monday last, forty-eight of these deluded men surrendered in a body to General Dundas, at Kilcullen, who commands in that district."

"Holt is confined in Dublin castle. According to the stipulations made previous to his surrender, he is shortly to set off for North America."—Courier Newspaper, 19th November, 1798.

[16 Nov 1798]

Dublin, November 16th.

"Different reports are in circulation respecting the nature of Holt's discoveries, some say that he had discovered the names of several persons of consequence in the county of Wicklow,* others, that he has given information of such persons in Dublin as had sent arms and ammunition to the Wicklow insurgents; but the most to be credited is this, that he is gone down with a strong detachment of the Royal army, this morning, to point out to them where the plunder taken from the Loyalists (amounting to several thousand pounds) had been secreted.**"—Courier Newspaper, 20th November, 1798.

"The Dublin Journal contradicts the report of Holt having been sent into the county of Wicklow, under a military escort, with a view of his making some useful discoveries. 'We can assure our readers (says that paper) there is no foundation for such a report, and that Holt still remains in close confinement.' "—Courier Newspaper, 22d November, 1798.

"The description given of the rebel leader, Holt, would apply to some greater men.—He is said to be an athletic ill-looking ruffian, with no feature of dignity or sagacity in his countenance."—Courier Newspaper, 23d November, 1798.

[22 November 1798]

Dublin, November 22.

"A fire broke out this morning at the Vice-regal Lodge, in the Phoenix Park, where His Excellency, the Marquis of Cornwallis, has been resident for some time; it originated in the offices attached to the dwelling-house, and burned some hay, but by the timely arrival of the fire-engines, was soon got under."*—Courier Newspaper, 27th November, 1798.

[* This is probably the fire mentioned by Holt at page 317.]

Dublin, November 24.

"The famous rebel Hacket has been brought to life again and re-killed. The following is an extract of a letter from Arklow of the 22d instant:—'We were in hopes, some time ago, that Hacket (who next after Holt had been most mischievous) had been taken, but upon examination of the body, supposed to be Hacket's, it turned out that it was that of a deserter, called Antrim Jack. Last night, however. Hacket met his fate; he, and three others, had the audacity to attack the house of Mr. Atkins, (Lord Carys-fort's agent) near this town; the fire from Mr. Atkins's house killed Hacket, and his followers ran away. We have just, now the satisfaction of seeing the traitor's body carried into the town; it is perfectly recognised.' "—Courier Newspaper, 28th November, 1798.

Page 366

Dublin, Monday, November 26.

"Holt, the rapparee leader, instead of being sent as a guide of a military force to the county of Wicklow, as stated in several of the morning papers, is a close prisoner iu the Castle of Dublin."—Hibernian Telegraph.

"The death of Holt's colleague and fellow-plunderer. Hacket, who was killed by Captain Atkinson of the Arklow Yeomanry, in an attack on his house, has restored tranquillity to that part of the county, from the depredations and outrages of desperate freebooters. The body of Hacket remained suspended from a tree, at the entrance of Arklow, on Thursday and Friday,"—Ibid,—Courier Newspaper, 1st December, 1798.

[January 1799]

"Holt, double-bolted, handcuffed, and encompassed with what is called an iron circumference, was lately put on board a transport to be conveyed to Botany Bay. He swore, blasphemed, and inveighed most bitterly against 'breach of treaty.'"—Courier Newspaper, 16th January, 1799.

Dublin, 4th March, 1799.

It is strongly rumoured that Holt, the late rebel chief of the army of Wicklow, whose very useful discoveries had induced government to spare his life, and only transport him, had been wrecked on the coast of Wicklow, and had joined several of his former rebel associates. But this, though certain persons are very active in circulating it, I cannot think is the case. I was lately in the county of Wicklow, and there understood that Holt had given information of the greatest importance to government; that he had informed against several very, wealthy farmers, &c. &c. It may be true that he has been shipwrecked on the coast of Wicklow; but I think it very unlikely that the man who had informed against so many of his associates, would be entrusted to take the command of those that escaped."—-Courier Newspaper, 9th March, 1799.

Mr. Cuthbert Hornidge's losses by the rebellion were estimated at £4510 1s. 8½d., and those of Mr. Richard Hornidge at £2395 5s. 8d. The losses of Mr. Charles Coates, in the county of Wicklow, at this period, see page 196, were estimated at £2139 4s. 6.; and the losses of— ——, see page 186 (which to the curious reader may supply a key), at £2773 5s. ld. The loss sustained by the Marquis of Waterford, see page 230, was estimated at £1637 18s. 5½d.
